href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<p></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Names_of_Bengal" title="Names of Bengal">Names of Bengal</a></div>\n<p>The etymology of <i>Bangladesh</i> (Country of Bengal) can be traced to the early 20th century, when Bengali patriotic songs, such as <i>Namo Namo Namo Bangladesh Momo</i> by <a href="/wiki/Kazi_Nazrul_Islam" title="Kazi Nazrul Islam">Kazi Nazrul Islam</a> and <i>Aaji Bangladesher Hridoy</i> by <a href="/wiki/Rabindranath_Tagore" title="Rabindranath Tagore">Rabindranath Tagore</a>, used the term.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> The term <i>Bangladesh</i> was often written as two words, <i>Bangla Desh</i>, in the past. Starting in the 1950s, Bengali nationalists used the term in political rallies in <a href="/wiki/East_Pakistan" title="East Pakistan">East Pakistan</a>. The term <i>Bangla</i> is a major name for both the <a href="/wiki/Bengal" title="Bengal">Bengal</a> region and the <a href="/wiki/Bengali_language" title="Bengali language">Bengali language</a>. The earliest known usage of the term is the <a href="/wiki/Nesari" title="Nesari">Nesari plate</a> in 805&#160;AD. The term <i>Vangaladesa</i> is found in 11th century South Indian records.<sup id="cite_ref-auto1_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-auto2_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto2-17">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-auto3_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-18">[18]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The term gained official status during the <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Bengal" class="mw-redirect" title="Sultanate of Bengal">Sultanate of Bengal</a> in the 14th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Ahmed2004_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ahmed2004-19">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Shamsuddin_Ilyas_Shah" title="Shamsuddin Ilyas Shah">Shamsuddin Ilyas Shah</a> proclaimed himself as the first "<a href="/wiki/Shah" title="Shah">Shah</a> of Bangala" in 1342.<sup id="cite_ref-Ahmed2004_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ahmed2004-19">[19]</a></sup> The word <i>Bangla</i> became the most common name for the region during the Islamic period. The <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_people" title="Portuguese people">Portuguese</a> referred to the region as <i>Bengala</i> in the 16th century.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The origins of the term <i>Bangla</i> are unclear, with theories pointing to a <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> <a href="/wiki/Proto-Dravidian_language" title="Proto-Dravidian language">proto-Dravidian</a> tribe,<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Austric" class="mw-redirect" title="Austric">Austric</a> word "Bonga" (Sun god),<sup id="cite_ref-auto4_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto4-23">[23]</a></sup> and the Iron Age <a href="/wiki/Vanga_Kingdom" title="Vanga Kingdom">Vanga Kingdom</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-auto4_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto4-23">[23]</a></sup> The Indo-Aryan suffix <i>Desh</i> is derived from the Sanskrit word <i>de\xc5\x9bha</i>, which means "land" or "country". Hence, the name <i>Bangladesh</i> means "Land of Bengal" or "Country of Bengal".<sup id="cite_ref-auto1_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-auto2_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto2-17">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-auto3_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-18">[18]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Bangladesh" title="History of Bangladesh">History of Bangladesh</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_and_medieval_periods">Early and medieval periods</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coin_of_Rajabhata_LACMA_M.77.55.1_(2_of_2).jpg" class="image"><img alt="See caption" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Coin_of_Rajabhata_LACMA_M.77.55.1_%282_of_2%29.jpg/220px-Coin_of_Rajabhata_LACMA_M.77.55.1_%282_of_2%29.jpg" width="220" height="192" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Coin_of_Rajabhata_LACMA_M.77.55.1_%282_of_2%29.jpg/330px-Coin_of_Rajabhata_LACMA_M.77.55.1_%282_of_2%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Coin_of_Rajabhata_LACMA_M.77.55.1_%282_of_2%29.jpg/440px-Coin_of_Rajabhata_LACMA_M.77.55.1_%282_of_2%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2100" data-file-height="1829" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coin_of_Rajabhata_LACMA_M.77.55.1_(2_of_2).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nGold coin (about 670 CE) from the reign of King Rajabhata of the Khadga dynasty</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Stone_Age" title="Stone Age">Stone Age</a> tools found in Bangladesh indicate human habitation for over 20,000 years,<sup id="cite_ref-Bharadwaj2003_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bharadwaj2003-24">[24]</a></sup> and remnants of <a href="/wiki/Copper_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="Copper Age">Copper Age</a> settlements date back 4,000 years.<sup id="cite_ref-Bharadwaj2003_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bharadwaj2003-24">[24]</a></sup> Ancient Bengal was settled by <a href="/wiki/Austroasiatic" class="mw-redirect" title="Austroasiatic">Austroasiatics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tibeto-Burman" class="mw-redirect" title="Tibeto-Burman">Tibeto-Burmans</a>, Dravidians and <a href="/wiki/Rigvedic_tribes" class="mw-redirect" title="Rigvedic tribes">Indo-Aryans</a> in consecutive waves of migration.<sup id="cite_ref-congress_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-congress-25">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bharadwaj2003_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bharadwaj2003-24">[24]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">Archaeological evidence</a> confirms that by the second millennium BCE, <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a>-cultivating communities inhabited the region. By the 11th century people lived in systemically-aligned housing, buried their dead, and manufactured copper ornaments and black and red pottery.<sup id="cite_ref-google5_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google5-26">[26]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ganges" title="Ganges">Ganges</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brahmaputra" class="mw-redirect" title="Brahmaputra">Brahmaputra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Meghna" class="mw-redirect" title="Meghna">Meghna</a> rivers were natural arteries for communication and transportation,<sup id="cite_ref-google5_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google5-26">[26]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Estuary" title="Estuary">estuaries</a> on the Bay of Bengal permitted <a href="/wiki/Maritime_industry" class="mw-redirect" title="Maritime industry">maritime</a> trade. The early <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> saw the development of metal weaponry, <a href="/wiki/Coin" title="Coin">coinage</a>, agriculture and <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-google5_26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google5-26">[26]</a></sup> Major urban settlements formed during the late Iron Age, in the mid-<a href="/wiki/First_millennium_BCE" class="mw-redirect" title="First millennium BCE">first millennium BCE</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> when the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Black_Polished_Ware" title="Northern Black Polished Ware">Northern Black Polished Ware</a> culture developed.<sup id="cite_ref-PierisRaven2010_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PierisRaven2010-28">[28]</a></sup> In 1879, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Cunningham" title="Alexander Cunningham">Alexander Cunningham</a> identified <a href="/wiki/Mahasthangarh" title="Mahasthangarh">Mahasthangarh</a> as the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Pundra_Kingdom" title="Pundra Kingdom">Pundra Kingdom</a> mentioned in the <i><a href="/wiki/Rigveda" title="Rigveda">Rigveda</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Greco-Roman_world" title="Greco-Roman world">Greek and Roman</a> records of the ancient <a href="/wiki/Gangaridai" title="Gangaridai">Gangaridai</a> Kingdom, which (according to legend) deterred the invasion of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>, are linked to the fort city in <a href="/wiki/Wari-Bateshwar_ruins" title="Wari-Bateshwar ruins">Wari-Bateshwar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-thedailystar1_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thedailystar1-31">[31]</a></sup> The site is also identified with the prosperous trading center of Souanagoura listed on <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy%27s_world_map" title="Ptolemy\'s world map">Ptolemy\'s world map</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-google1_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google1-32">[32]</a></sup> Roman geographers noted a large <a href="/wiki/Seaport" class="mw-redirect" title="Seaport">seaport</a> in southeastern Bengal, corresponding to the present-day <a href="/wiki/Chittagong_District" title="Chittagong District">Chittagong</a> region.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Ancient <a href="/wiki/Buddhist" class="mw-redirect" title="Buddhist">Buddhist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> states which ruled Bangladesh included the <a href="/wiki/Vanga_Kingdom" title="Vanga Kingdom">Vanga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samatata" title="Samatata">Samatata</a> and Pundra kingdoms, the <a href="/wiki/Maurya_Empire" title="Maurya Empire">Maurya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gupta_Empire" title="Gupta Empire">Gupta Empires</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Varman_dynasty" title="Varman dynasty">Varman dynasty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shashanka" title="Shashanka">Shashanka</a>\'s kingdom, the <a href="/wiki/Khadga_dynasty" title="Khadga dynasty">Khadga</a> and <a href="/wiki/Candra_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Candra dynasty">Candra dynasties</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Pala_Empire" title="Pala Empire">Pala Empire</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Sena_dynasty" title="Sena dynasty">Sena dynasty</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Harikela" title="Harikela">Harikela</a> kingdom and the <a href="/wiki/Deva_dynasty" title="Deva dynasty">Deva dynasty</a>. These states had well-developed currencies, banking, shipping, architecture and art, and the ancient universities of <a href="/wiki/Bikrampur" title="Bikrampur">Bikrampur</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mainamati" title="Mainamati">Mainamati</a> hosted scholars and students from other parts of Asia. <a href="/wiki/Xuanzang" title="Xuanzang">Xuanzang</a> of China was a noted scholar who resided at the <a href="/wiki/Somapura_Mahavihara" title="Somapura Mahavihara">Somapura Mahavihara</a> (the largest monastery in ancient India), and <a href="/wiki/Atisa" class="mw-redirect" title="Atisa">Atisa</a> traveled from Bengal to <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibet</a> to preach Buddhism. The earliest form of the <a href="/wiki/Bengali_language" title="Bengali language">Bengali language</a> began to the emerge during the eighth century.</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamization">Islamization</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Shait_Gumbad_Mosque_(28116770771).jpg" class="image"><img alt="Exterior of a low mosque with many domes and entrances" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Shait_Gumbad_Mosque_%2828116770771%29.jpg/220px-Shait_Gumbad_Mosque_%2828116770771%29.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Shait_Gumbad_Mosque_%2828116770771%29.jpg/330px-Shait_Gumbad_Mosque_%2828116770771%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Shait_Gumbad_Mosque_%2828116770771%29.jpg/440px-Shait_Gumbad_Mosque_%2828116770771%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Shait_Gumbad_Mosque_(28116770771).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nThe 15th-century <a href="/wiki/Sixty_Dome_Mosque" title="Sixty Dome Mosque">Sixty Dome Mosque</a> built during the Bengal Sultanate is now a <a href="/wiki/UNESCO_World_Heritage_Site" class="mw-redirect" title="UNESCO World Heritage Site">UNESCO World Heritage Site</a></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Atia_terracotta.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Atia_terracotta.jpg/220px-Atia_terracotta.jpg" width="220" height="368" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Atia_terracotta.jpg/330px-Atia_terracotta.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4e/Atia_terracotta.jpg 2x" data-file-width="359" data-file-height="600" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Atia_terracotta.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nThe wall carvings on the 17th-century Atia Mosque built during the Mughal Empire</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Early Muslim explorers and missionaries arrived in Bengal late in the first millennium CE. The Islamic conquest of Bengal began with the 1204 invasion by <a href="/wiki/Bakhtiar_Khilji" class="mw-redirect" title="Bakhtiar Khilji">Bakhtiar Khilji</a>; after annexing Bengal to the <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Sultanate" title="Delhi Sultanate">Delhi Sultanate</a>, Khilji waged a <a href="/wiki/Islamic_invasion_of_Tibet" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic invasion of Tibet">military campaign in Tibet</a>. Bengal was ruled by the Delhi Sultanate for a century by governors from the <a href="/wiki/Khilji_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Khilji dynasty">Khilji</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mamluk_dynasty_(Delhi)" title="Mamluk dynasty (Delhi)">Mamluk</a>, Balban and <a href="/wiki/Tughluq_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Tughluq dynasty">Tughluq dynasties</a>. During the 14th century, an independent <a href="/wiki/Bengal_Sultanate" title="Bengal Sultanate">Bengal Sultanate</a> was established by rebel governors. The sultanate\'s ruling houses included the <a href="/wiki/Ilyas_Shahi_dynasty" title="Ilyas Shahi dynasty">Ilyas Shahi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jalaluddin_Muhammad_Shah" title="Jalaluddin Muhammad Shah">Jalaluddin Muhammad Shah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hussain_Shahi_dynasty" title="Hussain Shahi dynasty">Hussain Shahi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Suri_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Suri dynasty">Suri</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karrani_dynasty" title="Karrani dynasty">Karrani dynasties</a>, and the era saw the introduction of a distinct mosque architecture<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_taka" title="History of the taka">tangka currency</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Arakan" title="Arakan">Arakan</a> region was brought under Bengali hegemony. The Bengal Sultanate was visited by explorers <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Battuta" title="Ibn Battuta">Ibn Battuta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Admiral_Zheng_He" class="mw-redirect" title="Admiral Zheng He">Admiral Zheng He</a> and <a href="/wiki/Niccolo_De_Conti" class="mw-redirect" title="Niccolo De Conti">Niccolo De Conti</a>. During the late 16th century, the <a href="/wiki/Baro-Bhuyan" title="Baro-Bhuyan">Baro-Bhuyan</a> (a confederation of Muslim and Hindu aristocrats) ruled eastern Bengal; its leader was the Mansad-e-Ala,<sup id="cite_ref-en.banglapedia.org_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-en.banglapedia.org-14">[14]</a></sup> a title held by <a href="/wiki/Isa_Khan" title="Isa Khan">Isa Khan</a> and his son <a href="/wiki/Musa_Khan" class="mw-redirect" title="Musa Khan">Musa Khan</a>. The Khan dynasty are considered local heroes for resisting North Indian invasions with their river navies.</p>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal Empire</a> controlled Bengal by the 17th century. During the reign of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Akbar" class="mw-redirect" title="Emperor Akbar">Emperor Akbar</a>, the Bengali agrarian calendar was reformed to facilitate tax collection. The Mughals established Dhaka as a fort city and commercial metropolis, and it was the capital of <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Bengal" class="mw-redirect" title="Mughal Bengal">Mughal Bengal</a> for 75 years.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> In 1666, the Mughals expelled the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Mrauk_U" title="Kingdom of Mrauk U">Arakanese</a> from Chittagong. Mughal Bengal attracted foreign traders for its <a href="/wiki/Muslin" title="Muslin">muslin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silk</a> goods, and the <a href="/wiki/Armenians_in_Bangladesh" title="Armenians in Bangladesh">Armenians</a> were a notable merchant community. A <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_settlement_in_Chittagong" title="Portuguese settlement in Chittagong">Portuguese settlement in Chittagong</a> flourished in the southeast, and a <a href="/wiki/Dutch_settlement_in_Rajshahi" title="Dutch settlement in Rajshahi">Dutch settlement in Rajshahi</a> existed in the north. During the 18th century, the <a href="/wiki/Nawabs_of_Bengal" class="mw-redirect" title="Nawabs of Bengal">Nawabs of Bengal</a> became the region\'s <i>de facto</i> rulers. The Nawabs forged alliances with European colonial companies, which made the region relatively prosperous early in the century.</p>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bengali_Muslim" class="mw-redirect" title="Bengali Muslim">Bengali Muslim</a> population was a product of conversion and religious evolution,<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> and their pre-Islamic beliefs included elements of Buddhism and Hinduism. The construction of mosques, Islamic academies (madrasas) and Sufi monasteries (<a href="/wiki/Khanqah" title="Khanqah">khanqahs</a>) facilitated conversion, and <a href="/wiki/Islamic_cosmology" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic cosmology">Islamic cosmology</a> played a significant role in developing Bengali Muslim society. Scholars have theorized that Bengalis were attracted to Islam by its egalitarian social order, which contrasted with the Hindu caste system.<sup id="cite_ref-Roy1999_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roy1999-37">[37]</a></sup> By the 15th century, Muslim poets were writing in the Bengali language. Notable medieval Bengali Muslim poets included <a href="/wiki/Daulat_Qazi" title="Daulat Qazi">Daulat Qazi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Hakim_(poet)" title="Abdul Hakim (poet)">Abdul Hakim</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alaol" title="Alaol">Alaol</a>. <a href="/wiki/Syncretic" class="mw-redirect" title="Syncretic">Syncretic</a> cults, such as the <a href="/wiki/Baul" title="Baul">Baul</a> movement, emerged on the fringes of Bengali Muslim society. The <a href="/wiki/Persianate" class="mw-redirect" title="Persianate">Persianate</a> culture was significant in Bengal, where cities like <a href="/wiki/Sonargaon" title="Sonargaon">Sonargaon</a> became the easternmost centers of Persian influence.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="British_Empire"><span id="British_Empire_period"></span>British Empire</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Presidencies_and_provinces_of_British_India" title="Presidencies and provinces of British India">Presidencies and provinces of British India</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British Raj</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Bengal_and_Assam" title="Eastern Bengal and Assam">Eastern Bengal and Assam</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Legislatures_of_British_India" title="Legislatures of British India">Legislatures of British India</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Muslimleague.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Large group photo" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/84/Muslimleague.jpg/220px-Muslimleague.jpg" width="220" height="71" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/84/Muslimleague.jpg/330px-Muslimleague.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/84/Muslimleague.jpg/440px-Muslimleague.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="257" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Muslimleague.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nThe 1906 All India Muhammadan Educational Conference in Dacca, <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Bengal_and_Assam" title="Eastern Bengal and Assam">Eastern Bengal and Assam</a></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>After the 1757 <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Plassey" title="Battle of Plassey">Battle of Plassey</a>, Bengal was the first region of the Indian subcontinent conquered by the <a href="/wiki/British_East_India_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="British East India Company">British East India Company</a>. The company formed the <a href="/wiki/Bengal_Presidency" title="Bengal Presidency">Presidency of Fort William</a>, which administered the region until 1858. A notable aspect of <a href="/wiki/Company_rule_in_India" title="Company rule in India">company rule</a> was the <a href="/wiki/Permanent_Settlement" title="Permanent Settlement">Permanent Settlement</a>, which established the feudal <a href="/wiki/Zamindar" title="Zamindar">zamindari</a> system. A number of famines, including the <a href="/wiki/Great_Bengal_famine_of_1770" title="Great Bengal famine of 1770">great Bengal famine of 1770</a>, occurred under company rule. Several rebellions broke out during the early 19th century (including one led by <a href="/wiki/Titumir" title="Titumir">Titumir</a>), but British rule displaced the Muslim ruling class. A conservative Islamic cleric, <a href="/wiki/Haji_Shariatullah" title="Haji Shariatullah">Haji Shariatullah</a>, sought to overthrow the British by propagating Islamic revivalism. Several towns in Bangladesh participated in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Mutiny" class="mw-redirect" title="Indian Mutiny">Indian Mutiny</a> and pledged allegiance to the last Mughal emperor, <a href="/wiki/Bahadur_Shah_Zafar" title="Bahadur Shah Zafar">Bahadur Shah Zafar</a>, who was later exiled to neighboring Burma.</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bengal_PMs.png" class="image"><img alt="Three Bengali Prime Ministers" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Bengal_PMs.png/220px-Bengal_PMs.png" width="220" height="91" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Bengal_PMs.png/330px-Bengal_PMs.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Bengal_PMs.png/440px-Bengal_PMs.png 2x" data-file-width="1458" data-file-height="600" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bengal_PMs.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Bengal" title="Prime Minister of Bengal">Prime Ministers of Bengal</a> <a href="/wiki/A._K._Fazlul_Huq" title="A. K. Fazlul Huq">A. K. Fazlul Huq</a>, <a href="/wiki/Khawaja_Nazimuddin" title="Khawaja Nazimuddin">Khawaja Nazimuddin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Huseyn_Shaheed_Suhrawardy" title="Huseyn Shaheed Suhrawardy">H. S. Suhrawardy</a></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The challenge posed to company rule by the failed Indian Mutiny led to the creation of the <a href="/wiki/British_Indian_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="British Indian Empire">British Indian Empire</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Crown_colony" title="Crown colony">crown colony</a>, and the first <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Bengal_Railway" title="Eastern Bengal Railway">railway</a> was built in 1862.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Syed_Ahmed_Khan" class="mw-redirect" title="Syed Ahmed Khan">Syed Ahmed Khan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ram_Mohan_Roy" title="Ram Mohan Roy">Ram Mohan Roy</a> promoted modern and <a href="/wiki/Liberal_education" title="Liberal education">liberal education</a> on the subcontinent, inspiring the <a href="/wiki/Aligarh_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Aligarh movement">Aligarh movement</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bengal_Renaissance" class="mw-redirect" title="Bengal Renaissance">Bengal Renaissance</a>. During the late 19th century, novelists, social reformers and feminists emerged from Muslim Bengali society. <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">Electricity</a> and municipal water systems were introduced in the 1890s; <a href="/wiki/Movie_theater" title="Movie theater">cinemas</a> opened in many towns during the early 20th century. East Bengal\'s <a href="/wiki/Plantation_economy" title="Plantation economy">plantation economy</a> was important to the British Empire, particularly its <a href="/wiki/Jute" title="Jute">jute</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tea_production_in_Bangladesh" title="Tea production in Bangladesh">tea</a>. The British established <a href="/wiki/Free_port" class="mw-redirect" title="Free port">tax-free river ports</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Port_of_Narayanganj" title="Port of Narayanganj">Port of Narayanganj</a>, and large seaports like the <a href="/wiki/Port_of_Chittagong" title="Port of Chittagong">Port of Chittagong</a>.</p>\n<p>Social tensions also increased under British rule, particularly between wealthy Hindus and the Muslim-majority population. The Permanent Settlement made millions of Muslim peasants tenants of Hindu estates, and resentment of the Hindu landed gentry grew.<sup id="cite_ref-Chatterjee2010_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chatterjee2010-41">[41]</a></sup> Supported by the Muslim aristocracy, the British government created the province of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Bengal_and_Assam" title="Eastern Bengal and Assam">Eastern Bengal and Assam</a> in 1905; the new province received increased investment in education, transport and industry. However, the <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_Bengal_(1905)" title="Partition of Bengal (1905)">first partition of Bengal</a> created an uproar in <a href="/wiki/Calcutta" class="mw-redirect" title="Calcutta">Calcutta</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Indian_National_Congress" title="Indian National Congress">Indian National Congress</a>. In response to growing Hindu nationalism, the <a href="/wiki/All_India_Muslim_League" class="mw-redirect" title="All India Muslim League">All India Muslim League</a> was formed in Dhaka during the 1906 All India Muhammadan Educational Conference. The British government reorganized the provinces in 1912, reuniting East and West Bengal and making <a href="/wiki/Assam_Province" title="Assam Province">Assam</a> a second province.</p>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tagore_and_Sher-e-Bangla.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A. K. Fazlul Huq and a bearded Rabindranath Tagore talking" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Tagore_and_Sher-e-Bangla.jpg/220px-Tagore_and_Sher-e-Bangla.jpg" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Tagore_and_Sher-e-Bangla.jpg/330px-Tagore_and_Sher-e-Bangla.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Tagore_and_Sher-e-Bangla.jpg/440px-Tagore_and_Sher-e-Bangla.jpg 2x" data-file-width="612" data-file-height="344" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tagore_and_Sher-e-Bangla.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nA. K. Fazlul Huq, the first prime minister of Bengal, with Nobel laureate <a href="/wiki/Rabindranath_Tagore" title="Rabindranath Tagore">Rabindranath Tagore</a></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The Raj was slow to allow self-rule in the colonial subcontinent. It established the <a href="/wiki/Bengal_Legislative_Council" title="Bengal Legislative Council">Bengal Legislative Council</a> in 1862, and the council\'s native Bengali representation increased during the early 20th century. The <a href="/wiki/Bengal_Provincial_Muslim_League" title="Bengal Provincial Muslim League">Bengal Provincial Muslim League</a> was formed in 1913 to advocate <a href="/wiki/Civil_rights" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil rights">civil rights</a> for Bengali Muslims within a constitutional framework. During the 1920s, the league was divided into factions supporting the <a href="/wiki/Khilafat_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Khilafat movement">Khilafat movement</a> and favoring cooperation with the British to achieve self-rule. Segments of the Bengali elite supported <a href="/wiki/Mustafa_Kemal_Ataturk" class="mw-redirect" title="Mustafa Kemal Ataturk">Mustafa Kemal Ataturk</a> <a href="/wiki/Secularism_in_Turkey" title="Secularism in Turkey">secularist</a> forces.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> In 1929, the <a href="/wiki/Praja_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="Praja Party">All Bengal Tenants Association</a> was formed in the Bengal Legislative Council to counter the influence of the Hindu landed gentry, and the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Independence_Movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Indian Independence Movement">Indian Independence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_Movement" title="Pakistan Movement">Pakistan Movements</a> strengthened during the early 20th century. After the <a href="/wiki/Morley-Minto_Reforms" class="mw-redirect" title="Morley-Minto Reforms">Morley-Minto Reforms</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_India_Act,_1919" title="Government of India Act, 1919">diarchy</a> era in the <a href="/wiki/Legislatures_of_British_India" title="Legislatures of British India">legislatures of British India</a>, the British government promised <a href="/wiki/Government_of_India_Act,_1935" title="Government of India Act, 1935">limited provincial autonomy</a> in 1935. The <a href="/wiki/Bengal_Legislative_Assembly" title="Bengal Legislative Assembly">Bengal Legislative Assembly</a>, British India\'s largest legislature, was established in 1937.</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Working_Committee.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Group photo outside a building" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Working_Committee.jpg/220px-Working_Committee.jpg" width="220" height="134" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Working_Committee.jpg/330px-Working_Committee.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Working_Committee.jpg/440px-Working_Committee.jpg 2x" data-file-width="458" data-file-height="278" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Working_Committee.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nThe All India Muslim League\'s <a href="/wiki/Lahore_Resolution" title="Lahore Resolution">Lahore Resolution</a> Working Committee, in which Bengal was represented by A. K. Fazlul Huq and Khawaja Nazimuddin</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Although it won a majority of seats in 1937, the Bengal Congress boycotted the legislature. <a href="/wiki/A._K._Fazlul_Huq" title="A. K. Fazlul Huq">A. K. Fazlul Huq</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Krishak_Sramik_Party" title="Krishak Sramik Party">Krishak Praja Party</a> was elected as the first <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Bengal" title="Prime Minister of Bengal">Prime Minister of Bengal</a>. In 1940 Huq supported the <a href="/wiki/Lahore_Resolution" title="Lahore Resolution">Lahore Resolution</a>, which envisaged independent states in the northwestern and eastern Muslim-majority regions of the subcontinent. The first Huq ministry, a coalition with the Bengal Provincial Muslim League, lasted until 1941; it was followed by a Huq coalition with the <a href="/wiki/Hindu_Mahasabha" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindu Mahasabha">Hindu Mahasabha</a> which lasted until 1943. Huq was succeeded by <a href="/wiki/Khawaja_Nazimuddin" title="Khawaja Nazimuddin">Khawaja Nazimuddin</a>, who grappled with the effects of the <a href="/wiki/Burma_Campaign" title="Burma Campaign">Burma Campaign</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Bengal_famine_of_1943" title="Bengal famine of 1943">Bengal famine of 1943</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Quit_India" class="mw-redirect" title="Quit India">Quit India</a> movement. In 1946, the Bengal Provincial Muslim League won the provincial election, taking 113 of the 250-seat assembly (the largest Muslim League mandate in British India). <a href="/wiki/H._S._Suhrawardy" class="mw-redirect" title="H. S. Suhrawardy">H. S. Suhrawardy</a>, who made a final futile effort for a <a href="/wiki/United_Bengal" title="United Bengal">United Bengal</a> in 1946, was the last premier of Bengal.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Partition_of_Bengal">Partition of Bengal</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_Bengal_(1947)" title="Partition of Bengal (1947)">Partition of Bengal (1947)</a></div>\n<p>The 3 June 1947 <a href="/wiki/Mountbatten_Plan" class="mw-redirect" title="Mountbatten Plan">Mountbatten Plan</a> outlined the <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_British_India" class="mw-redirect" title="Partition of British India">partition of British India</a>. On 20 June, the Bengal Legislative Assembly met to decide on the partition of Bengal. At the preliminary joint meeting, it was decided (120 votes to 90) that if the province remained united it should join the <a href="/wiki/Constituent_Assembly_of_Pakistan" title="Constituent Assembly of Pakistan">Constituent Assembly of Pakistan</a>. At a separate meeting of legislators from <a href="/wiki/West_Bengal" title="West Bengal">West Bengal</a>, it was decided (58 votes to 21) that the province should be partitioned and West Bengal should join the <a href="/wiki/Constituent_Assembly_of_India" title="Constituent Assembly of India">Constituent Assembly of India</a>. At another meeting of legislators from <a href="/wiki/East_Bengal" title="East Bengal">East Bengal</a>, it was decided (106 votes to 35) that the province should not be partitioned and (107 votes to 34) that East Bengal should join the Constituent Assembly of Pakistan if Bengal was partitioned.<sup id="cite_ref-Mukherjee1987_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mukherjee1987-43">[43]</a></sup> On 6 July, the <a href="/wiki/Sylhet_Division" title="Sylhet Division">Sylhet region</a> of Assam voted in a <a href="/wiki/Sylhet_referendum,_1947" title="Sylhet referendum, 1947">referendum to join East Bengal</a>.</p>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Cyril_Radcliffe" class="mw-redirect" title="Cyril Radcliffe">Cyril Radcliffe</a> was tasked with drawing the borders of Pakistan and India, and the <a href="/wiki/Radcliffe_Line" title="Radcliffe Line">Radcliffe Line</a> established the borders of present-day Bangladesh. The <a href="/wiki/Dominion_of_Pakistan" title="Dominion of Pakistan">Dominion of Pakistan</a> was created on 14 August 1947.</p>\n<h3><span id="Union_with_Pakistan_(1947\xe2\x80\x931971)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Union_with_Pakistan_.281947.E2.80.931971.29">Union with Pakistan (1947\xe2\x80\x931971)</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/East_Bengal" title="East Bengal">East Bengal</a> and <a href="/wiki/East_Pakistan" title="East Pakistan">East Pakistan</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dominion_of_Pakistan_%26_Indian_Controlled_Kashmir_(orthographic_projection).svg" class="image"><img alt="Map of the world, with Pakistan in 1947 highlighted" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Dominion_of_Pakistan_%26_Indian_Controlled_Kashmir_%28orthographic_projection%29.svg/220px-Dominion_of_Pakistan_%26_Indian_Controlled_Kashmir_%28orthographic_projection%29.svg.png" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Dominion_of_Pakistan_%26_Indian_Controlled_Kashmir_%28orthographic_projection%29.svg/330px-Dominion_of_Pakistan_%26_Indian_Controlled_Kashmir_%28orthographic_projection%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Dominion_of_Pakistan_%26_Indian_Controlled_Kashmir_%28orthographic_projection%29.svg/440px-Dominion_of_Pakistan_%26_Indian_Controlled_Kashmir_%28orthographic_projection%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="541" data-file-height="541" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dominion_of_Pakistan_%26_Indian_Controlled_Kashmir_(orthographic_projection).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nThe <a href="/wiki/Dominion_of_Pakistan" title="Dominion of Pakistan">Dominion of Pakistan</a> in 1947, with <a href="/wiki/East_Bengal" title="East Bengal">East Bengal</a> its eastern part</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>East Bengal, with Dhaka its capital, was the most populous province of the 1947 <a href="/wiki/State_of_Pakistan" class="mw-redirect" title="State of Pakistan">Pakistani federation</a> (led by <a href="/wiki/Governor_General_of_Pakistan" class="mw-redirect" title="Governor General of Pakistan">Governor General</a> <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_Jinnah" title="Muhammad Ali Jinnah">Muhammad Ali Jinnah</a>, who promised freedom of religion and secular democracy in the new state).<sup id="cite_ref-Ispahani2017_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ispahani2017-44">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Saikia2011_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Saikia2011-45">[45]</a></sup> East Bengal was also Pakistan\'s most cosmopolitan province, home to peoples of different faiths, cultures and ethnic groups. Partition gave increased economic opportunity to East Bengalis, producing an urban population during the 1950s.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Khawaja_Nazimuddin" title="Khawaja Nazimuddin">Khawaja Nazimuddin</a> was East Bengal\'s first <a href="/wiki/Chief_Minister_of_East_Bengal" class="mw-redirect" title="Chief Minister of East Bengal">chief minister</a> with <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Chalmers_Bourne" title="Frederick Chalmers Bourne">Frederick Chalmers Bourne</a> its governor. The <a href="/wiki/All_Pakistan_Awami_Muslim_League" title="All Pakistan Awami Muslim League">All Pakistan Awami Muslim League</a> was formed in 1949 as a centre-left alternative to the centre-right All Pakistan Muslim League.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In 1950, the <a href="/wiki/East_Bengal_Legislative_Assembly" class="mw-redirect" title="East Bengal Legislative Assembly">East Bengal Legislative Assembly</a> enacted <a href="/wiki/Land_reform" title="Land reform">land reform</a>, abolishing the Permanent Settlement and the <a href="/wiki/Zamindar" title="Zamindar">zamindari</a> system.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> The 1952 <a href="/wiki/Bengali_Language_Movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Bengali Language Movement">Bengali Language Movement</a> was the first sign of friction between the country\'s geographically-separated wings. The Awami Muslim League was renamed the more-secular <a href="/wiki/Awami_League" class="mw-redirect" title="Awami League">Awami League</a> in 1953.<sup id="cite_ref-LewisSagar1992_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LewisSagar1992-49">[49]</a></sup> The first constituent assembly was dissolved in 1954; this was challenged by its East Bengali speaker, <a href="/wiki/Maulvi_Tamizuddin_Khan" title="Maulvi Tamizuddin Khan">Maulvi Tamizuddin Khan</a>. The <a href="/wiki/United_Front_(East_Pakistan)" title="United Front (East Pakistan)">United Front</a> coalition swept aside the Muslim League in a landslide victory in the 1954 <a href="/wiki/East_Bengali_legislative_election,_1954" title="East Bengali legislative election, 1954">East Bengali legislative election</a>. The following year, East Bengal was renamed East Pakistan as part of the <a href="/wiki/One_Unit" title="One Unit">One Unit</a> program and the province became a vital part of the <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia_Treaty_Organization" title="Southeast Asia Treaty Organization">Southeast Asia Treaty Organization</a>.</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:21_Feb_1953_Dhaka_University_female_students_procession.png" class="image"><img alt="Female students, dressed in white, march down a street." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/21_Feb_1953_Dhaka_University_female_students_procession.png/220px-21_Feb_1953_Dhaka_University_female_students_procession.png" width="220" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/21_Feb_1953_Dhaka_University_female_students_procession.png/330px-21_Feb_1953_Dhaka_University_female_students_procession.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/21_Feb_1953_Dhaka_University_female_students_procession.png/440px-21_Feb_1953_Dhaka_University_female_students_procession.png 2x" data-file-width="1374" data-file-height="1056" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:21_Feb_1953_Dhaka_University_female_students_procession.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nFemale students march in defiance of the <a href="/wiki/Section_144" class="mw-redirect" title="Section 144">Section 144</a> prohibition on assembly during the Bengali Language Movement in early 1953.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Pakistan adopted its first constitution in 1956. Three Bengalis were its Prime Minister until 1957: Nazimuddin, <a href="/wiki/Mohammad_Ali_of_Bogra" class="mw-redirect" title="Mohammad Ali of Bogra">Mohammad Ali of Bogra</a> and Suhrawardy. None of the three completed their terms, and resigned from office. The <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_Army" title="Pakistan Army">Pakistan Army</a> imposed <a href="/wiki/1958_Pakistani_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1958 Pakistani coup d\'\xc3\xa9tat">military rule in 1958</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ayub_Khan_(general)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ayub Khan (general)">Ayub Khan</a> was the country\'s strongman for 11 years. Political repression increased after the coup. Khan introduced a new constitution in 1962, replacing Pakistan\'s parliamentary system with a presidential and gubernatorial system (based on <a href="/wiki/Electoral_college" title="Electoral college">electoral college</a> selection) known as Basic Democracy. In 1962 Dhaka became the seat of the <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_of_Pakistan" title="National Assembly of Pakistan">National Assembly of Pakistan</a>, a move seen as appeasing increased Bengali nationalism.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> The Pakistani government built the controversial <a href="/wiki/Kaptai_Dam" title="Kaptai Dam">Kaptai Dam</a>, displacing the <a href="/wiki/Chakma_people" title="Chakma people">Chakma people</a> from their indigenous homeland in the <a href="/wiki/Chittagong_Hill_Tracts" title="Chittagong Hill Tracts">Chittagong Hill Tracts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Pakistani_presidential_election,_1965" title="Pakistani presidential election, 1965">1965 presidential election</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fatima_Jinnah" title="Fatima Jinnah">Fatima Jinnah</a> lost to Ayub Khan despite support from the Combined Opposition alliance (which included the Awami League).<sup id="cite_ref-Ahmed2004p157_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ahmed2004p157-52">[52]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Indo-Pakistani_War_of_1965" title="Indo-Pakistani War of 1965">Indo-Pakistani War of 1965</a> blocked cross-border transport links with neighboring India in what is described as a second partition.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> In 1966, Awami League leader <a href="/wiki/Sheikh_Mujibur_Rahman" title="Sheikh Mujibur Rahman">Sheikh Mujibur Rahman</a> announced a <a href="/wiki/Six_point_movement" title="Six point movement">six point movement</a> for a federal parliamentary democracy.</p>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Maulvi_Tamizuddin_Khan_and_US_Chief_Justice.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Earl Warren and Maulvi Tamizuddin Khan shake hands as a third man looks on" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4a/Maulvi_Tamizuddin_Khan_and_US_Chief_Justice.jpg" width="200" height="257" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="200" data-file-height="257" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Maulvi_Tamizuddin_Khan_and_US_Chief_Justice.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_the_United_States" title="Chief Justice of the United States">U.S. Chief Justice</a> <a href="/wiki/Earl_Warren" title="Earl Warren">Earl Warren</a> meets Maulvi Tamizuddin Khan, the plaintiff in <i><a href="/wiki/Federation_of_Pakistan_v._Maulvi_Tamizuddin_Khan" title="Federation of Pakistan v. Maulvi Tamizuddin Khan">Federation of Pakistan v. Maulvi Tamizuddin Khan</a></i>.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>According to senior <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a> officials, Pakistan practiced extensive <a href="/wiki/Economic_discrimination" title="Economic discrimination">economic discrimination</a> against East Pakistan: greater government spending on West Pakistan, financial transfers from East to West Pakistan and the use of East Pakistan\'s foreign-exchange surpluses to finance West Pakistani imports;<sup id="cite_ref-google.co.nz_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google.co.nz-54">[54]</a></sup> East Pakistan generated 70 percent of Pakistan\'s export revenue with its jute and tea.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Sheikh Mujibur Rahman was arrested for treason in the <a href="/wiki/Agartala_Conspiracy_Case" title="Agartala Conspiracy Case">Agartala Conspiracy Case</a>, and was released during the <a href="/wiki/1969_uprising_in_East_Pakistan" title="1969 uprising in East Pakistan">1969 uprising in East Pakistan</a> which resulted in Ayub Khan\'s resignation. General <a href="/wiki/Yahya_Khan" title="Yahya Khan">Yahya Khan</a> assumed power, reintroducing martial law.</p>\n<p>Ethnic and linguistic discrimination was common in Pakistan\'s civil and military services, in which Bengalis were under-represented. Fifteen percent of Pakistani central-government offices were occupied by East Pakistanis, who formed 10 percent of the military.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Cultural discrimination also prevailed, making East Pakistan forge a distinct political identity.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Pakistan banned Bengali literature and music in state media, including the works of Nobel laureate Rabindranath Tagore.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/1970_Bhola_cyclone" title="1970 Bhola cyclone">cyclone</a> devastated the coast of East Pakistan in 1970, killing an estimated 500,000 people,<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> and the central government was criticized for its poor response.<sup id="cite_ref-countrystudies.us1_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-countrystudies.us1-61">[61]</a></sup> After the December 1970 elections, calls for the independence of East Bengal became louder;<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> the Bengali-nationalist Awami League won 167 of 169 East Pakistani seats in the National Assembly. The League claimed the right to form a government and develop a new constitution, but was strongly opposed by the Pakistani military and the <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_Peoples_Party" title="Pakistan Peoples Party">Pakistan Peoples Party</a> (led by <a href="/wiki/Zulfikar_Ali_Bhutto" title="Zulfikar Ali Bhutto">Zulfikar Ali Bhutto</a>).</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="War_of_Independence">War of Independence</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh_Liberation_War" title="Bangladesh Liberation War">Bangladesh Liberation War</a></div>\n<p>The Bengali population was angered when <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister-elect" class="mw-redirect" title="Prime Minister-elect">Prime Minister-elect</a> Sheikh Mujibur Rahman was prevented from taking office.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Civil_disobedience" title="Civil disobedience">Civil disobedience</a> erupted across East Pakistan, with calls for independence.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Rahman addressed a <a href="/wiki/7th_March_Speech_of_Sheikh_Mujibur_Rahman" class="mw-redirect" title="7th March Speech of Sheikh Mujibur Rahman">pro-independence rally</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dhaka" title="Dhaka">Dacca</a> on 7 March 1971. The <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Bangladesh" title="Flag of Bangladesh">Bangladeshi flag</a> was raised for the first time on 23 March, Pakistan\'s <a href="/wiki/Republic_Day_(Pakistan)" class="mw-redirect" title="Republic Day (Pakistan)">Republic Day</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> During the night of 25 March, the Pakistani <a href="/wiki/Military_junta" class="mw-redirect" title="Military junta">military junta</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Yahya_Khan" title="Yahya Khan">Yahya Khan</a> launched <a href="/wiki/Operation_Searchlight" title="Operation Searchlight">Operation Searchlight</a> (a sustained military assault on East Pakistan)<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> and held Rahman in military custody.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_Army" title="Pakistan Army">Pakistan Army</a>, with help from supporting militias, massacred Bengali <a href="/wiki/1971_Dhaka_University_massacre" title="1971 Dhaka University massacre">students</a>, <a href="/wiki/1971_killing_of_Bengali_intellectuals" title="1971 killing of Bengali intellectuals">intellectuals</a>, politicians, civil servants and military defectors in the <a href="/wiki/1971_Bangladesh_genocide" title="1971 Bangladesh genocide">1971 Bangladesh genocide</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Several million refugees fled to neighboring India. Estimates of the number killed during the war range from 300,000 to three million.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Global public opinion turned against Pakistan as news of the atrocities spread;<sup id="cite_ref-google.com.bd1_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google.com.bd1-74">[74]</a></sup> the Bangladesh movement was supported by prominent political and cultural figures in the West, including <a href="/wiki/Ted_Kennedy" title="Ted Kennedy">Ted Kennedy</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Harrison" title="George Harrison">George Harrison</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bob_Dylan" title="Bob Dylan">Bob Dylan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joan_Baez" title="Joan Baez">Joan Baez</a>, <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Ocampo" title="Victoria Ocampo">Victoria Ocampo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andre_Malraux" class="mw-redirect" title="Andre Malraux">Andre Malraux</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Concert_for_Bangladesh" title="The Concert for Bangladesh">The Concert for Bangladesh</a> was held at <a href="/wiki/Madison_Square_Garden" title="Madison Square Garden">Madison Square Garden</a> in New York City to raise funds for Bangladeshi refugees. The first major <a href="/wiki/Benefit_concert" title="Benefit concert">benefit concert</a> in history, it was organized by Harrison and Indian Bengali sitarist <a href="/wiki/Ravi_Shankar" title="Ravi Shankar">Ravi Shankar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>\n<p>During the Bangladesh Liberation War, Bengali nationalists declared independence and formed the <a href="/wiki/Mukti_Bahini" title="Mukti Bahini">Mukti Bahini</a> (the Bangladeshi National Liberation Army). The <a href="/wiki/Provisional_Government_of_Bangladesh" title="Provisional Government of Bangladesh">Provisional Government of Bangladesh</a> was established on 17 April 1971, converting the 469 elected members of the Pakistani national assembly and East Pakistani provincial assembly into the <a href="/wiki/Constituent_Assembly_of_Bangladesh" title="Constituent Assembly of Bangladesh">Constituent Assembly of Bangladesh</a>. The provisional government issued the <a href="/wiki/Proclamation_of_Bangladeshi_Independence" title="Proclamation of Bangladeshi Independence">Proclamation of Bangladeshi Independence</a>, which was the country\'s interim constitution and declared "equality, human dignity and social justice" as its fundamental principles. Due to Rahman\'s detention, the acting president was <a href="/wiki/Syed_Nazrul_Islam" title="Syed Nazrul Islam">Syed Nazrul Islam</a>. <a href="/wiki/Tajuddin_Ahmad" title="Tajuddin Ahmad">Tajuddin Ahmad</a> was Bangladesh\'s first prime minister. The military wing of the provisional government was the <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh_Forces" class="mw-redirect" title="Bangladesh Forces">Bangladesh Forces</a>. Led by General <a href="/wiki/M._A._G._Osmani" title="M. A. G. Osmani">M. A. G. Osmani</a> and eleven <a href="/wiki/List_of_sectors_in_Bangladesh_Liberation_War" class="mw-redirect" title="List of sectors in Bangladesh Liberation War">sector commanders</a>, the forces held the Bengali countryside during the war and conducted wide-ranging <a href="/wiki/Guerrilla_war" class="mw-redirect" title="Guerrilla war">guerrilla operations</a> against Pakistani forces. Neighboring India and its leader, <a href="/wiki/Indira_Gandhi" title="Indira Gandhi">Indira Gandhi</a> (a longtime nemesis of Pakistan), provided crucial support to the Bangladesh Forces and intervened in support of the provisional government on 3 December 1971. The <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> and the United States dispatched naval forces to the Bay of Bengal in a <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> standoff during the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Pakistani_War_of_1971" title="Indo-Pakistani War of 1971">Indo-Pakistani War</a>. The nine-month war ended with the <a href="/wiki/Surrender_of_Pakistan" class="mw-redirect" title="Surrender of Pakistan">surrender of Pakistan\'s military</a> to the Bangladesh-India Allied Forces on 16 December 1971.<sup id="cite_ref-laporte_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-laporte-79">[79]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rummel-8-2_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rummel-8-2-80">[80]</a></sup> Under international pressure, Pakistan released Rahman from imprisonment on 8 January 1972 and he was flown by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a> to a million-strong homecoming in Dacca.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Indian troops were withdrawn by 12 March 1972, three months after the war ended.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The cause of Bangladeshi self-determination was recognized around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-google.com.bd1_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google.com.bd1-74">[74]</a></sup> By the time of its admission to UN membership in August 1972, the new state was recognized by 86 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-google.com.bd1_74-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google.com.bd1-74">[74]</a></sup> Pakistan recognized Bangladesh in 1974 after pressure from most of the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Muslim world</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="First_parliamentary_era">First parliamentary era</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mujib_and_Ford.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A seated Sheikh Mujibur Rahman and Gerald Ford, smiling and talking" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Mujib_and_Ford.jpg/220px-Mujib_and_Ford.jpg" width="220" height="133" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Mujib_and_Ford.jpg/330px-Mujib_and_Ford.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Mujib_and_Ford.jpg/440px-Mujib_and_Ford.jpg 2x" data-file-width="604" data-file-height="366" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mujib_and_Ford.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nPrime Minister Sheikh Mujibur Rahman and U.S. president <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Ford" title="Gerald Ford">Gerald Ford</a> in 1974</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The constituent assembly adopted Bangladesh\'s constitution on 4 November 1972, establishing a secular, multiparty parliamentary democracy. The new constitution included references to <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialism</a>, and Prime Minister Sheikh Mujibur Rahman <a href="/wiki/Nationalized" class="mw-redirect" title="Nationalized">nationalized</a> major industries in 1972.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> A major reconstruction and rehabilitation program was launched. The Awami League won the country\'s first general election in 1973, securing a large majority in the <a href="/wiki/Jatiyo_Sangshad" class="mw-redirect" title="Jatiyo Sangshad">Jatiyo Sangshad</a>. Bangladesh joined the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a>, the UN, the <a href="/wiki/Organisation_of_Islamic_Cooperation" title="Organisation of Islamic Cooperation">OIC</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Non-Aligned_Movement" title="Non-Aligned Movement">Non-Aligned Movement</a>, and Rahman strengthened ties with India. Amid growing agitation by the opposition <a href="/wiki/National_Awami_Party" title="National Awami Party">National Awami Party</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jashod" class="mw-redirect" title="Jashod">National Socialist Party</a>, he became increasingly authoritarian. Rahman amended the constitution, giving himself more emergency powers (including the suspension of fundamental rights). The <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh_famine_of_1974" title="Bangladesh famine of 1974">Bangladesh famine of 1974</a> also worsened the political situation.<sup id="cite_ref-Lewis2011_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis2011-86">[86]</a></sup></p>\n<h4><span id="Presidential_era_and_coups_(1975\xe2\x80\x931991)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidential_era_and_coups_.281975.E2.80.931991.29">Presidential era and coups (1975\xe2\x80\x931991)</span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Military_coups_in_Bangladesh" title="Military coups in Bangladesh">Military coups in Bangladesh</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dutch_Royal_Family_meets_Ziaur_Rahman_and_wife_1979.jpg" class="image"><img alt="President Ziaur Rahman and First Lady Khaleda Zia with six members of the Dutch royal family" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Dutch_Royal_Family_meets_Ziaur_Rahman_and_wife_1979.jpg/220px-Dutch_Royal_Family_meets_Ziaur_Rahman_and_wife_1979.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Dutch_Royal_Family_meets_Ziaur_Rahman_and_wife_1979.jpg/330px-Dutch_Royal_Family_meets_Ziaur_Rahman_and_wife_1979.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Dutch_Royal_Family_meets_Ziaur_Rahman_and_wife_1979.jpg/440px-Dutch_Royal_Family_meets_Ziaur_Rahman_and_wife_1979.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3680" data-file-height="2443" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dutch_Royal_Family_meets_Ziaur_Rahman_and_wife_1979.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nPresident Ziaur Rahman and First Lady Khaleda Zia with the Dutch royal family in 1979</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>In January 1975, Sheikh Mujibur Rahman introduced <a href="/wiki/One-party_state" title="One-party state">one-party socialist rule</a> under <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh_Krishak_Sramik_Awami_League" title="Bangladesh Krishak Sramik Awami League">BAKSAL</a>. Rahman banned all newspapers except four state-owned publications, and amended the constitution to increase his power. He was <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Sheikh_Mujibur_Rahman" title="Assassination of Sheikh Mujibur Rahman">assassinated</a> during a coup on 15 August 1975. <a href="/wiki/Martial_law" title="Martial law">Martial law</a> was declared, and the presidency passed to the <a href="/wiki/Usurper" title="Usurper">usurper</a> <a href="/wiki/Khondaker_Mostaq_Ahmad" title="Khondaker Mostaq Ahmad">Khondaker Mostaq Ahmad</a> for four months. Ahmad is widely regarded as a <a href="/wiki/Quisling" title="Quisling">quisling</a> by Bangladeshis.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Tajuddin Ahmad, the nation\'s first prime minister, and four other independence leaders were assassinated on 4 November 1975. Chief Justice <a href="/wiki/Abu_Sadat_Mohammad_Sayem" title="Abu Sadat Mohammad Sayem">Abu Sadat Mohammad Sayem</a> was installed as president by the military on 6 November 1975. Bangladesh was governed by a <a href="/wiki/Military_junta" class="mw-redirect" title="Military junta">military junta</a> led by the <a href="/wiki/Chief_Martial_Law_Administrator" title="Chief Martial Law Administrator">Chief Martial Law Administrator</a> for three years. In 1977, Army chief <a href="/wiki/Ziaur_Rahman" title="Ziaur Rahman">Ziaur Rahman</a> became president. Rahman reinstated <a href="/wiki/Multiparty" class="mw-redirect" title="Multiparty">multiparty</a> politics, <a href="/wiki/Privatization" title="Privatization">privatized</a> industries and newspapers, established <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh_Export_Processing_Zone_Authority" title="Bangladesh Export Processing Zone Authority">BEPZA</a> and held the country\'s second general election in 1979. A <a href="/wiki/Semi-presidential" class="mw-redirect" title="Semi-presidential">semi-presidential</a> system evolved, with the <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh_Nationalist_Party" title="Bangladesh Nationalist Party">Bangladesh Nationalist Party</a> (BNP) governing until 1982. Rahman was assassinated in 1981, and was succeeded by <a href="/wiki/Vice_President_of_Bangladesh" title="Vice President of Bangladesh">Vice President</a> <a href="/wiki/Abdus_Sattar_(president)" title="Abdus Sattar (president)">Abdus Sattar</a>. Sattar received 65.5 percent of the vote in the <a href="/wiki/Bangladeshi_presidential_election,_1981" title="Bangladeshi presidential election, 1981">1981 presidential election</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Khasru_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Khasru-88">[88]</a></sup></p>\n<p>After a year in office, Sattar was overthrown in the <a href="/wiki/1982_Bangladesh_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1982 Bangladesh coup d\'\xc3\xa9tat">1982 Bangladesh coup d\'\xc3\xa9tat</a>. Chief Justice <a href="/wiki/A._F._M._Ahsanuddin_Chowdhury" title="A. F. M. Ahsanuddin Chowdhury">A. F. M. Ahsanuddin Chowdhury</a> was installed as president, but army chief <a href="/wiki/Hussain_Muhammad_Ershad" title="Hussain Muhammad Ershad">Hussain Muhammad Ershad</a> became the country\'s <i>de facto</i> leader and assumed the presidency in 1983. Ershad lifted martial law in 1986. He governed with four successive prime ministers (<a href="/wiki/Ataur_Rahman_Khan" title="Ataur Rahman Khan">Ataur Rahman Khan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mizanur_Rahman_Chowdhury" title="Mizanur Rahman Chowdhury">Mizanur Rahman Chowdhury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moudud_Ahmed" title="Moudud Ahmed">Moudud Ahmed</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kazi_Zafar_Ahmed" title="Kazi Zafar Ahmed">Kazi Zafar Ahmed</a>) and a parliament dominated by his <a href="/wiki/Jatiya_Party_(Ershad)" title="Jatiya Party (Ershad)">Jatiyo Party</a>. General elections were held in 1986 and 1988, although the latter was boycotted by the opposition BNP and Awami League. Ershad pursued administrative decentralization, dividing the country into 64 districts, and pushed Parliament to make Islam the state religion in 1988.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/1990_Mass_Uprising_in_Bangladesh" title="1990 Mass Uprising in Bangladesh">1990 mass uprising</a> forced him to resign, and Chief Justice <a href="/wiki/Shahabuddin_Ahmed" title="Shahabuddin Ahmed">Shahabuddin Ahmed</a> led the country\'s first caretaker government as part of the transition to parliamentary rule.<sup id="cite_ref-Khasru_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Khasru-88">[88]</a></sup></p>\n<h4><span id="Current_parliamentary_era_(1991\xe2\x80\x93present)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_parliamentary_era_.281991.E2.80.93present.29">Current parliamentary era (1991\xe2\x80\x93present)</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:National_Assembly_of_Bangladesh_(19018585420).jpg" class="image"><img alt="Large, modern building on landscaped grounds" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/National_Assembly_of_Bangladesh_%2819018585420%29.jpg/220px-National_Assembly_of_Bangladesh_%2819018585420%29.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/National_Assembly_of_Bangladesh_%2819018585420%29.jpg/330px-National_Assembly_of_Bangladesh_%2819018585420%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/National_Assembly_of_Bangladesh_%2819018585420%29.jpg/440px-National_Assembly_of_Bangladesh_%2819018585420%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2736" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:National_Assembly_of_Bangladesh_(19018585420).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nThe <a href="/wiki/Jatiyo_Sangshad_Bhaban" class="mw-redirect" title="Jatiyo Sangshad Bhaban">Parliament of Bangladesh</a>, completed in 1982, is one of the world\'s largest legislative complexes.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>After the 1991 general election, the twelfth amendment to the constitution restored the parliamentary republic and <a href="/wiki/Begum_Khaleda_Zia" class="mw-redirect" title="Begum Khaleda Zia">Begum Khaleda Zia</a> became Bangladesh\'s first female prime minister. Zia, a former first lady, led a BNP government from 1990 to 1996. In 1991 her finance minister, <a href="/wiki/Saifur_Rahman" title="Saifur Rahman">Saifur Rahman</a>, began a major program to liberalize the Bangladeshi economy.<sup id="cite_ref-Lewis2011_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis2011-86">[86]</a></sup></p>\n<p>After an Awami League initiative, the BNP introduced a system of <a href="/wiki/Caretaker_government_of_Bangladesh" title="Caretaker government of Bangladesh">caretaker governments</a> to oversee the transfer of power. Justice <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Habibur_Rahman" title="Muhammad Habibur Rahman">Muhammad Habibur Rahman</a> was the first <a href="/wiki/Chief_Adviser_of_Bangladesh" class="mw-redirect" title="Chief Adviser of Bangladesh">Chief Adviser of Bangladesh</a>, and oversaw the 1996 election. The Awami League, led by <a href="/wiki/Sheikh_Hasina" title="Sheikh Hasina">Sheikh Hasina</a>, won the seventh general election. Hasina\'s first term was highlighted by the <a href="/wiki/Chittagong_Hill_Tracts_Peace_Accord" title="Chittagong Hill Tracts Peace Accord">Chittagong Hill Tracts Peace Accord</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Ganges" title="Ganges">Ganges</a> water-sharing treaty with India. The second caretaker government, led by Chief Adviser Justice <a href="/wiki/Latifur_Rahman" title="Latifur Rahman">Latifur Rahman</a>, oversaw the eighth general election in 2001 which returned Begum Zia and the BNP to power. The second Zia ministry saw improved economic growth, but political turmoil gripped the country between 2004 and 2006. A radical Islamist militant group, the <a href="/wiki/Jamaat-ul-Mujahideen_Bangladesh" title="Jamaat-ul-Mujahideen Bangladesh">JMB</a>, carried out a series of bombings. Amid widespread political unrest the Bangladeshi military urged President <a href="/wiki/Iajuddin_Ahmed" title="Iajuddin Ahmed">Iajuddin Ahmed</a> to impose a <a href="/wiki/State_of_emergency" title="State of emergency">state of emergency</a> and a caretaker government, led by technocrat <a href="/wiki/Fakhruddin_Ahmed" title="Fakhruddin Ahmed">Fakhruddin Ahmed</a>, was installed.<sup id="cite_ref-Lewis2011_86-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis2011-86">[86]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Emergency rule lasted for two years, until the ninth general election in 2008 which returned Sheikh Hasina and the Awami League to power. In 2010, the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_Bangladesh" title="Supreme Court of Bangladesh">Supreme Court</a> ruled martial law illegal and affirmed <a href="/wiki/Secularism" title="Secularism">secular</a> principles in the constitution. The following year, the Awami League abolished the caretaker-government system. The 2014 general election was boycotted by the BNP, giving the Awami League a decisive victory.</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Bangladesh" title="Geography of Bangladesh">Geography of Bangladesh</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bg-map.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Bg-map.png/220px-Bg-map.png" width="220" height="236" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/46/Bg-map.png 1.5x" data-file-width="330" data-file-height="354" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bg-map.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nA map of Bangladesh</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The geography of Bangladesh is divided between three regions. Most of the country is dominated by the fertile <a href="/wiki/Ganges-Brahmaputra_delta" class="mw-redirect" title="Ganges-Brahmaputra delta">Ganges-Brahmaputra delta</a>; the northwest and central parts of the country are formed by the <a href="/wiki/Madhupur_tract" title="Madhupur tract">Madhupur</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Barind_Tract" title="Barind Tract">Barind</a> plateaus. The northeast and southeast are home to <a href="/wiki/Evergreen" title="Evergreen">evergreen</a> hill ranges. The Ganges delta is formed by the confluence of the Ganges (local name <a href="/wiki/Padma_River" title="Padma River">Padma</a> or <i>P\xc3\xb4dda</i>), <a href="/wiki/Brahmaputra_River" title="Brahmaputra River">Brahmaputra</a> (<a href="/wiki/Jamuna_River_(Bangladesh)" title="Jamuna River (Bangladesh)">Jamuna</a> or <i>Jomuna</i>), and <a href="/wiki/Meghna_River" title="Meghna River">Meghna</a> rivers and their respective tributaries. The Ganges unites with the Jamuna (main channel of the Brahmaputra) and later joins the Meghna, finally flowing into the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Bengal" title="Bay of Bengal">Bay of Bengal</a>. Bangladesh has 57 <a href="/wiki/Trans-boundary_river" class="mw-redirect" title="Trans-boundary river">trans-boundary rivers</a>, making the resolution of water issues to be politically complicated, in most cases, as the country is a lower <a href="/wiki/Riparian_zone" title="Riparian zone">riparian</a> state to India.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Bangladesh is predominately rich fertile flat land. Most parts of it is less than 12&#160;m (39.4&#160;ft) above sea level, and it is estimated that about 10% of its land would be flooded if the sea level were to rise by 1&#160;m (3.28&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-ali_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ali-91">[91]</a></sup> 17% of the country is covered by forests and 12% is covered by hill systems. The country\'s <a href="/wiki/Haor" title="Haor">haor</a> wetlands are of significance to global environmental science.</p>\n<p>In southeastern Bangladesh, experiments have been done since the 1960s to \'build with nature\'. Construction of cross dams has induced a natural accretion of silt, creating new land. With Dutch funding, the Bangladeshi government began promoting the development of this new land in the late 1970s. The effort has become a multi-agency endeavor, building roads, culverts, embankments, cyclone shelters, toilets and ponds, as well as distributing land to settlers. It was expected that by fall 2010, the program would have allotted some 27,000 acres (10,927&#160;ha) to 21,000 families.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> With an elevation of 1,064&#160;m (3,491&#160;ft), the highest peak of Bangladesh is <a href="/wiki/Keokradong" title="Keokradong">Keokradong</a>, near the border with <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a>.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administrative_geography">Administrative geography</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Administrative_geography_of_Bangladesh" title="Administrative geography of Bangladesh">Administrative geography of Bangladesh</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote title="Districts of Bangladesh">Districts of Bangladesh</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Upazilas_of_Bangladesh" title="Upazilas of Bangladesh">Upazilas of Bangladesh</a></div>\n<div class="mw-stack //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Bangladesh_divisions_english.svg/600px-Bangladesh_divisions_english.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1550" data-file-height="2150" usemap="#ImageMap_1_266549830" />\n<div style="margin-left: 280px; margin-top: -415px; text-align: left;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bangladesh_divisions_english.svg" title="About this image"><img alt="About this image" src="/w/extensions/ImageMap/desc-20.png?15600" style="border: none;" /></a></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Bangladesh is divided into eight administrative divisions,<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CIA_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-94">[94]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> each named after their respective divisional headquarters: <a href="/wiki/Barisal_Division" title="Barisal Division">Barisal</a>, Chittagong, <a href="/wiki/Dhaka_Division" title="Dhaka Division">Dhaka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Khulna_Division" title="Khulna Division">Khulna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mymensingh_Division" title="Mymensingh Division">Mymensingh</a>, Rajshahi, Rangpur, and Sylhet.</p>\n<p>Divisions are subdivided into districts (<i>zila</i>). There are 64 districts in Bangladesh, each further subdivided into <i>upazila</i> (subdistricts) or <i>thana</i>. The area within each police station, except for those in metropolitan areas, is divided into several <i><a href="/wiki/Union_Councils_of_Bangladesh" class="mw-redirect" title="Union Councils of Bangladesh">unions</a></i>, with each union consisting of multiple villages. In the metropolitan areas, police stations are divided into wards, which are further divided into <i>mahallas</i>.</p>\n<p>There are no elected officials at the divisional or district levels, and the administration is composed only of government officials. Direct elections are held in each union (or ward) for a chairperson and a number of members. In 1997, a parliamentary act was passed to reserve three seats (out of 12) in every union for female candidates.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>\n<table class="wikitable sortable">\n<tr>\n<th colspan="8">Administrative Divisions of Bangladesh</th>\n</tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;
